<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">RF resonant cavity thruster</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="hatnote" role="note">"EM Drive" redirects here. For electromagnetic spacecraft drives, see <a href="/wiki/Electrically_powered_spacecraft_propulsion" title="Electrically powered spacecraft propulsion">Electrically powered spacecraft propulsion</a>. For electromagnetic terrestrial drives, see <a href="/wiki/Electric_motor" title="Electric motor">electric motor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Linear_motor" title="Linear motor">linear motor</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Induction_motor" title="Induction motor">induction motor</a>.</div>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em">
<caption>RF resonant cavity thruster</caption>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color: gainsboro; font-weight: bold;">Controversial invention</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EmDrive_built_by_Eagleworks_inside_the_test_chamber.jpg"><img alt="EmDrive built by Eagleworks inside the test chamber.jpg" data-file-height="408" data-file-width="437" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/EmDrive_built_by_Eagleworks_inside_the_test_chamber.jpg/220px-EmDrive_built_by_Eagleworks_inside_the_test_chamber.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/EmDrive_built_by_Eagleworks_inside_the_test_chamber.jpg/330px-EmDrive_built_by_Eagleworks_inside_the_test_chamber.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/10/EmDrive_built_by_Eagleworks_inside_the_test_chamber.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div>Prototype resonant cavity thruster built by <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eagleworks_Laboratories" title="Eagleworks Laboratories">Eagleworks Laboratories</a></div>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Inventor</th>
<td>Roger Shawyer (EmDrive), Guido Fetta (Cannae Drive)</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Theory violation</th>
<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservation_of_momentum" title="Conservation of momentum">Conservation of momentum</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newton%27s_Third_Law" title="Newton's Third Law">Newton's Third Law</a></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>A <b>radio frequency</b> (<b>RF</b>) <b>resonant cavity thruster</b> is a controversial proposed type of <a href="/wiki/Electrically_powered_spacecraft_propulsion" title="Electrically powered spacecraft propulsion">electromagnetic thruster</a> where an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anisotropic" title="Anisotropic">anisotropic</a> <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_field" title="Electromagnetic field">electromagnetic field</a> inside the <a href="/wiki/Microwave_cavity" title="Microwave cavity">microwave cavity</a> purportedly transfers <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a> to the cavity producing <a href="/wiki/Thrust" title="Thrust">thrust</a>.</p>
<p>Conventional <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Thruster" title="Thruster">thrusters</a> expel <a href="/wiki/Propellant" title="Propellant">propellant</a>, such as when ships move masses of water, aircraft move masses of air, or rockets expel exhaust products. A drive which does not expel propellant in order to produce a <a href="/wiki/Reaction_(physics)" title="Reaction (physics)">reaction force</a>, but rather provides thrust from the electromagnetic field without any external interaction, is a <a href="/wiki/Reactionless_drive" title="Reactionless drive">reactionless drive</a>. Such a <a href="/wiki/Closed_system" title="Closed system">closed system</a> need not carry propellant and hence would be capable of always producing thrust, as long as it is powered, and would appear to violate the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservation_of_momentum" title="Conservation of momentum">conservation of momentum</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newton%27s_third_law" title="Newton's third law">Newton's third law</a>, leading many physicists to believe such thrusters to be impossible, labeling them as <a href="/wiki/Pseudoscience" title="Pseudoscience">pseudoscience</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nat_Geo_Peer_review_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nat_Geo_Peer_review-1">[1]</a></sup> Conversely, if the thrust is due to an interaction with an external <a href="/wiki/Field_(physics)" title="Field (physics)">field</a>, the drive is then an <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_system#Open_system" title="Thermodynamic system">open system</a>, still <a href="/wiki/Field_propulsion" title="Field propulsion">propellantless</a> but not reactionless, like a conventional spacecraft accelerates at the expense of the momentum of the planet it orbits during a <a href="/wiki/Gravity_assist" title="Gravity assist">gravity assist maneuver</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slingshot_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-slingshot-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite the lack of theoretical consensus as to how such devices can work inventors continue to try to develop such drives because of the possibility of supporting long voyages in space, where propellant is a primary limiting factor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wired_2009_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wired_2009-3">[3]</a></sup> Roger Shawyer published a design with a tapered conical cavity, which he called the EmDrive. Guido Fetta later published a design with a pillbox cavity, which he called the Cannae Drive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AW.26ST_2012_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-AW.26ST_2012-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wired_2013_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wired_2013-5">[5]</a></sup> A few groups of physicists have tried to build and test their own thrusters, based on the designs published by Shawyer and Fetta. Juan Yang at <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an" title="Xi'an">Xi'an</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Northwestern_Polytechnical_University" title="Northwestern Polytechnical University">Northwestern Polytechnical University</a> (NWPU) initially reported thrust,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wired_2008_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wired_2008-6">[6]</a></sup> but retracted her claims in 2016 after a measurement error was identified and an improved setup measured no significant thrust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yang_2016_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yang_2016-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Harold_G._White" title="Harold G. White">Harold White</a>'s group at NASA's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eagleworks_Laboratories" title="Eagleworks Laboratories">Eagleworks Laboratories</a>, which tests unusual rocket designs, tested a version of these designs. In 2015, they ran a test run that observed thrust of 40–100 μN from inputs of 40–80 W. Their paper was published in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Journal_of_Propulsion_and_Power" title="Journal of Propulsion and Power">Journal of Propulsion and Power</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eagleworks_AIAA_2016_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eagleworks_AIAA_2016-9">[9]</a></sup> At a press conference in Beijing on 10 December 2016 held by the <a href="/wiki/China_Academy_of_Space_Technology" title="China Academy of Space Technology">China Academy of Space Technology</a> (CAST) Dr Yue Chen, head of the communication satellite division at CAST, confirmed the agency is already testing an EmDrive in <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a> onboard an "experimental verification platform". It stated it has successfully measured a thrust onboard laboratory conditions and plans to add the technology to Chinese satellites as soon as possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>This concept for a thruster drew attention in the 2000s when a few popular science magazines wrote articles about it as an "impossible" drive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-New_Scientist_blog_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-New_Scientist_blog-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-powell-no_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-powell-no-15">[15]</a></sup> There was clear criticism of misleading claims in the media<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> that the drive had been "validated by NASA" following White's first test reports in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-powell-no_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-powell-no-15">[15]</a></sup> The lack of an unbiased treatment in the media, from both polarized sides, has been raised by <a href="/wiki/Breakthrough_Propulsion_Physics_Program#Legacy" title="Breakthrough Propulsion Physics Program">Tau Zero Foundation</a>'s scientist reviewers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tau_Zero_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tau_Zero-17">[17]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_and_context">History and context</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=RF_resonant_cavity_thruster&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History and context">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_propulsion" title="Electromagnetic propulsion">Electromagnetic propulsion</a> designs which operate on the principle of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reaction_mass" title="Reaction mass">reaction mass</a> have been around since the start of the 20th century. In the 1960s, extensive research was conducted on two designs which emit high velocity ionized gases in similar ways: <a href="/wiki/Ion_thruster" title="Ion thruster">ion thrusters</a> that convert propellant to ions and accelerate and eject them via <a href="/wiki/Electric_potential" title="Electric potential">electric potentials</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Plasma_propulsion_engine" title="Plasma propulsion engine">plasma thrusters</a> that convert propellant to plasma ions and accelerate and eject them via <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a> currents. In the latter, plasma can be generated from an intense source of microwave or other radio-frequency (RF) energy, and in combination with a resonant cavity, can be tuned to resonate at a precise frequency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>A low-propellant space drive has long been a goal for space exploration, since the propellant is dead weight that must be lifted and accelerated with the ship all the way from launch until the moment it is used (see <a href="/wiki/Tsiolkovsky_rocket_equation" title="Tsiolkovsky rocket equation">Tsiolkovsky rocket equation</a>). <a href="/wiki/Gravity_assist" title="Gravity assist">Gravity assists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_sail" title="Solar sail">Solar sails</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Beam-powered_propulsion" title="Beam-powered propulsion">beam-powered propulsion</a> from a spacecraft-remote location such as the ground or in orbit, are useful because they allow a ship to gain speed without propellant. However, some of these methods do not work in deep space. Shining a light out of the ship provides a small force from <a href="/wiki/Radiation_pressure" title="Radiation pressure">radiation pressure</a>, i.e., using photons as a form of propellant, but the force is far too weak (for a given amount of input power) to be useful in practice.</p>
<p>A true zero-propellant drive is widely believed to be impossible, but if it existed, it could potentially be used for travel in many environments including deep space. Thus, such drives are a popular concept in science fiction, and their improbability contributes to enthusiasm for exploring such designs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wired_2009_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wired_2009-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AW.26ST_2012_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-AW.26ST_2012-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wired_2013_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wired_2013-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversy">Controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=RF_resonant_cavity_thruster&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Controversy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The design of such thrusters and the theories that attempt to explain how they might work are all matters of controversy, more so claims that it is an example of a <a href="/wiki/Reactionless_drive" title="Reactionless drive">reactionless drive</a>, in violation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservation_of_momentum" title="Conservation of momentum">conservation of momentum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wired_2013_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wired_2013-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-forbes_tucker_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-forbes_tucker-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/John_C._Baez" title="John C. Baez">John C. Baez</a>, a mathematical physicist at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Riverside" title="University of California, Riverside">University of California, Riverside</a>, and Australian science fiction writer <a href="/wiki/Greg_Egan" title="Greg Egan">Greg Egan</a> said that as of 2006 the positive results reported by Shawyer were likely misinterpretations of experimental errors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-egan_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-egan-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>Physicists Eric W. Davis at the Institute for Advanced Studies in Austin, and <a href="/wiki/Sean_M._Carroll" title="Sean M. Carroll">Sean M. Carroll</a> at the <a href="/wiki/California_Institute_of_Technology" title="California Institute of Technology">California Institute of Technology</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-21">[21]</a></sup> have said in 2015 that the thrust measured in both the Dresden University experiments and in earlier Eagleworks publications were indicative of thermal effect errors.</p>
<p>White's 2014 conference paper suggested that resonant cavity thrusters could work by transferring momentum to the "quantum vacuum virtual plasma".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_AIAA_2014_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_AIAA_2014-22">[22]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Baez" title="John Baez">Baez</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sean_M._Carroll" title="Sean M. Carroll">Carroll</a> criticized this explanation, because in the standard description of vacuum fluctuations, <a href="/wiki/Virtual_particle" title="Virtual particle">virtual particles</a> do not behave as a plasma; Carroll also noted that the quantum vacuum has no "rest frame", providing nothing to push against, so it can't be used for propulsion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Powell_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Powell-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>White's 2016 peer-reviewed paper<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eagleworks_AIAA_2016_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eagleworks_AIAA_2016-9">[9]</a></sup> invoked the <a href="/wiki/De_Broglie%E2%80%93Bohm_theory" title="De Broglie–Bohm theory">pilot-wave theory</a> to suggest how the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantum_vacuum" title="Quantum vacuum">quantum vacuum</a> could be used to generate thrust, however the paper noted that such interpretations are “not the dominant view of physics today.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nat_Geo_Peer_review_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nat_Geo_Peer_review-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Designs_and_prototypes">Designs and prototypes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=RF_resonant_cavity_thruster&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Designs and prototypes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Emdrive_schematic.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="384" data-file-width="512" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Emdrive_schematic.svg/220px-Emdrive_schematic.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Emdrive_schematic.svg/330px-Emdrive_schematic.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Emdrive_schematic.svg/440px-Emdrive_schematic.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Emdrive_schematic.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Simplified schematic drawing of an EmDrive prototype by Tajmar and Fiedler, according to Shawyer's model</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="EmDrive">EmDrive</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=RF_resonant_cavity_thruster&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: EmDrive">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2001, Shawyer founded <i>Satellite Propulsion Research Ltd</i>, in order to work on the EmDrive, a drive that he said used a resonant cavity to produce thrust without propellant. The company was backed by a "Smart Award" grant from the <a href="/wiki/Department_of_Trade_and_Industry_(United_Kingdom)" title="Department of Trade and Industry (United Kingdom)">UK Department of Trade and Industry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wired_2013_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-Wired_2013-5">[5]</a></sup> In December 2002, he described a working prototype with a total thrust of about 0.02 newtons powered by an 850 W <a href="/wiki/Cavity_magnetron" title="Cavity magnetron">cavity magnetron</a>. The device could operate for only a few dozen seconds before the magnetron failed, due to overheating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_device_and_New_Scientist_article">Second device and <i>New Scientist</i> article</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=RF_resonant_cavity_thruster&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Second device and New Scientist article">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In October 2006, Shawyer conducted tests on a new water-cooled prototype and said that it had increased thrust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eureka2007_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eureka2007-26">[26]</a></sup> He planned to have the device ready to use in space by May 2009 and was considering making the resonant cavity a superconductor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eureka2007_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eureka2007-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/New_Scientist" title="New Scientist">New Scientist</a></i> magazine<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shawyertheory_9.3_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-shawyertheory_9.3-27">[27]</a></sup> featured the EmDrive on the cover of 8 September 2006 issue. The article portrayed the device as plausible and emphasized the arguments of those who held that point of view. <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">Science fiction</a> author <a href="/wiki/Greg_Egan" title="Greg Egan">Greg Egan</a> distributed a public letter stating that "a sensationalist bent and a lack of basic knowledge by its writers" made the magazine's coverage unreliable, sufficient "to constitute a real threat to the public understanding of science". Especially, Egan said he was "gobsmacked by the level of scientific illiteracy" in the magazine's coverage, alleging that it used "meaningless double-talk" to obfuscate the problem of conservation of momentum. The letter was endorsed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mathematical_physicist" title="Mathematical physicist">mathematical physicist</a> <a href="/wiki/John_C._Baez" title="John C. Baez">John C. Baez</a> and posted on his blog.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-egan_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-egan-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Powell_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Powell-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Egan also recommended<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-egan_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-egan-20">[20]</a></sup> that <i>New Scientist</i> publish a refutation penned by John P. Costella (a data scientist with a PhD in <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_physics" title="Theoretical physics">theoretical physics</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shawyertheory_9.3_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-shawyertheory_9.3-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-costella_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-costella-28">[28]</a></sup> <i>New Scientist</i> editor Jeremy Webb responded to critics, stating,</p>
<blockquote>
<p>It is a fair criticism that <i>New Scientist</i> did not make clear enough how controversial Roger Shawyer’s engine is. We should have made more explicit where it apparently contravenes the laws of nature and reported that several physicists declined to comment on the device because they thought it too contentious<span class="nowrap"> </span>... The great thing is that Shawyer's ideas are testable. If he succeeds in getting his machine flown in space, we will know soon enough if it is ground-breaking device or a mere flight of fancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-New_Scientist_blog_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-New_Scientist_blog-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p><i>New Scientist</i> also published a letter from the former technical director of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EADS_Astrium" title="EADS Astrium">EADS Astrium</a>, who stated: "I reviewed Roger’s work and concluded that both theory and experiment were fatally flawed. Roger was advised that the company had no interest in the device, did not wish to seek patent coverage and in fact did not wish to be associated with it in any way",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newscientist-astrium-debunk_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-newscientist-astrium-debunk-29">[29]</a></sup> and a letter from physicist Paul Friedlander, who stated</p>
<blockquote>
<p>As I read it, I, like the thousands of other physicists who will have read it, immediately realised that this was impossible as described. Physicists are trained to use certain fundamental principles to analyse a problem and this claim clearly flouted one of them<span class="nowrap"> </span>... The Shawyer drive is as impossible as perpetual motion. Relativistic conservation of momentum has been understood for a century and dictates that if nothing emerges from Shawyer’s device then its centre of mass will not accelerate. It is likely that Shawyer has used an approximation somewhere in his calculations that would have been reasonable if he hadn’t then multiplied the result by 50,000. The reason physicists value principles such as conservation of momentum is that they act as a reality check against errors of this kind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_work">Later work</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=RF_resonant_cavity_thruster&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Later work">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 2007, the UK <a href="/wiki/Department_of_Trade_and_Industry_(United_Kingdom)" title="Department of Trade and Industry (United Kingdom)">Department of Trade and Industry</a> granted SPR an <a href="/wiki/International_Traffic_in_Arms_Regulations" title="International Traffic in Arms Regulations">ITAR</a> export licence to <a href="/wiki/Boeing" title="Boeing">Boeing</a> in the US.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> In December 2008, Shawyer was invited to <a href="/wiki/The_Pentagon" title="The Pentagon">The Pentagon</a> to make a presentation on the EmDrive, then <a href="/wiki/Boeing" title="Boeing">Boeing</a> confirmed they wanted to licence the technology. The UK <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Defence" title="Ministry of Defence">Ministry of Defence</a> agreed to a <a href="/wiki/Technology_transfer" title="Technology transfer">technology transfer</a>, and SPR designed, built and tested a Flight Thruster for use on a test satellite. According to Shawyer, the 10-month contract was completed by July 2010 and the Flight Thruster, giving 18 grams of thrust, transferred to Boeing. Afterwards, SPR never received a licence agreement and communication with Boeing stopped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IBT14102016_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-IBT14102016-32">[32]</a></sup> Questioned on that matter in 2012, a Boeing representative confirmed <a href="/wiki/Boeing_Phantom_Works" title="Boeing Phantom Works">Boeing Phantom Works</a> used to explore exotic forms of space propulsion including Shawyer's drive some years ago, but such work has since ceased, stating that "Phantom Works is not working with Mr. Shawyer,” and adding that the company is no longer pursuing this avenue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AW.26ST_2012_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-AW.26ST_2012-4">[4]</a></sup> No further details of Boeing's Flight Thruster have been made public.</p>
<p>In 2013 and 2014, Shawyer presented ideas for 'second-generation' EmDrive designs and applications, at the annual <a href="/wiki/International_Astronautical_Congress" title="International Astronautical Congress">International Astronautical Congress</a>. A paper based on his 2014 presentation was published in <i><a href="/wiki/Acta_Astronautica" title="Acta Astronautica">Acta Astronautica</a></i> in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shawyer_Acta_Astronautica_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shawyer_Acta_Astronautica-33">[33]</a></sup> It describes a model for a superconducting resonant cavity and three models for thrusters with multiple cavities, with hypothetical applications for launching space probes.</p>
<p>In October 2016, a UK patent application describing a new superconducting EmDrive was published,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IBT12102016_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-IBT12102016-34">[34]</a></sup> followed by a first international version.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-international_patent_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-international_patent-35">[35]</a></sup> Shortly thereafter Shawyer unveiled the creation of <i>Universal Propulsion Ltd.</i>, a new company aimed to develop and commercialise such thrusters, as a <a href="/wiki/Joint_venture" title="Joint venture">joint venture</a> with <i>Gilo Industries Group</i>, a small UK aerospace company designing and selling <a href="/wiki/Paramotor" title="Paramotor">paramotors</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Parajet_Skycar" title="Parajet Skycar">Parajet Skycar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IBT14102016_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-IBT14102016-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cannae_and_other_drives">Cannae and other drives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=RF_resonant_cavity_thruster&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Cannae and other drives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Cannae Drive (formerly Q-drive),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cannae_patent_1_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cannae_patent_1-36">[36]</a></sup> another engine designed to generate propulsion from a resonant cavity without propellant, is another implementation of this idea. Its cavity is also asymmetric, but relatively flat rather than a truncated cone. It was designed by Fetta in 2006 and has been promoted within the US through his company, Cannae LLC, since 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cannae_patent_1_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cannae_patent_1-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cannae_LLC_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cannae_LLC-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cannae_patent_2_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cannae_patent_2-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cannae_AIAA_2014_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cannae_AIAA_2014-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cannae_patent_3_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cannae_patent_3-40">[40]</a></sup> In 2016, Fetta announced plans to eventually launch a <a href="/wiki/CubeSat" title="CubeSat">cubesat</a> satellite containing a version of the Cannae Drive, which they would run for 6 months to observe how it functions in space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Researchers working under Dr Juan Yang at the <a href="/wiki/Northwestern_Polytechnical_University" title="Northwestern Polytechnical University">Northwestern Polytechnical University</a> (NWPU) in <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an" title="Xi'an">Xi'an</a> developed their own prototype resonant cavity thruster in 2008, publishing a report in their university's journal on the theory behind such devices. In 2012–2014 they reported measuring net thrust in a series of preliminary tests, however in 2014 they reported that this had been an experimental error introduced by a power cable. In a revised study with an improved model, they reported that any thrust generated was too small for their setup to measure (less than 1 mN for a 230 W power source).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AW.26ST_2012_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-AW.26ST_2012-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yang_2013_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yang_2013-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yang_2014_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yang_2014-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2016, Dr Yue Chen at <a href="/wiki/China_Academy_of_Space_Technology" title="China Academy of Space Technology">China Academy of Space Technology</a> (CAST) filed several patent applications describing various RF resonant cavity thruster designs, notably a mean to recursively stack several short resonant cavities to improve thrust,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CN105781921A_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-CN105781921A-44">[44]</a></sup> and a design based on a semicylinder instead of a frustum,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CN105947224A_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-CN105947224A-45">[45]</a></sup> before announcing tests in <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PopSci_20161220_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-PopSci_20161220-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Device_structure">Device structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=RF_resonant_cavity_thruster&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Device structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>All these devices use <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">microwaves</a>, produced by high power <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_tube" title="Vacuum tube">vacuum tubes</a> (like a <a href="/wiki/Cavity_magnetron" title="Cavity magnetron">cavity magnetron</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Traveling-wave_tube" title="Traveling-wave tube">traveling-wave tube</a> (TWTA)) or <a href="/wiki/Solid-state_electronics" title="Solid-state electronics">solid-state</a> <a href="/wiki/Field-effect_transistor" title="Field-effect transistor">field-effect transistor</a> RF generators, that are directed into a metallic, fully enclosed conically tapered high <i><a href="/wiki/Q_factor" title="Q factor">Q</a></i> resonant <a href="/wiki/Microwave_cavity" title="Microwave cavity">microwave cavity</a>. They have a greater area at one end of the device and, for some versions, a <a href="/wiki/Dielectric_resonator" title="Dielectric resonator">dielectric resonator</a> added in front of the narrower end. They require an electric power source to run the microwave generator, but no propellant.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Simulated_transverse_magnetic_modes_in_RF_cavity.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="758" data-file-width="1736" height="96" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Simulated_transverse_magnetic_modes_in_RF_cavity.png/220px-Simulated_transverse_magnetic_modes_in_RF_cavity.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Simulated_transverse_magnetic_modes_in_RF_cavity.png/330px-Simulated_transverse_magnetic_modes_in_RF_cavity.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Simulated_transverse_magnetic_modes_in_RF_cavity.png/440px-Simulated_transverse_magnetic_modes_in_RF_cavity.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Simulated_transverse_magnetic_modes_in_RF_cavity.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Simulated <a href="/wiki/Transverse_mode#Types_of_modes" title="Transverse mode">transverse magnetic modes</a> TM20, (red high, blue low) at the wide and narrow ends of a metal tapered cavity differ from each other, forming an anisotropic interference of electromagnetic waves<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GrahnAnnila2016_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-GrahnAnnila2016-47">[47]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The electromagnetic waves associated with the geometry of a truncated cone are very complex, creating an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anisotropic" title="Anisotropic">anisotropic</a> electromagnetic field. Unlike the geometry of a cylinder, there are no analytical solutions for the resonant modes of a truncated cone. This geometry intrinsically does not suffer from mode degeneration. A sharp distinction between the propagating and <a href="/wiki/Evanescent_field" title="Evanescent field">evanescent electromagnetic waves</a> cannot be achieved and as a result a non-zero power flow runs through truncated cone focusing a portion of energy near the apex. There is no well-defined cutoff wavelength but rather a cutoff radius. Due to the absence of sharp cut off wavelengths the interior of the truncated cone can support inhomogeneous field amplitudes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> There are many other situations where the electromagnetic field is believed to be anisotropic for example, doped active centers in anisotropic glasses,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RikkenKessener1995_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-RikkenKessener1995-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GlauberLewenstein1991_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-GlauberLewenstein1991-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ScheelKn.C3.B6ll1999_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-ScheelKn.C3.B6ll1999-54">[54]</a></sup> emission of active atoms in a waveguide,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrorsonSkovgaard1996_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-BrorsonSkovgaard1996-55">[55]</a></sup> spontaneous emission from atoms adsorbed on metallic or dielectric surfaces,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Agarwal1975_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Agarwal1975-56">[56]</a></sup> emission in a spatially dispersive medium<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HorsleyPhilbin2014_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-HorsleyPhilbin2014-57">[57]</a></sup> which allows the possibility of <a href="/wiki/Longitudinal_wave#Electromagnetic" title="Longitudinal wave">longitudinal electromagnetic fields</a> (classically not found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_space" title="Free space">free space</a> but in substances like <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasmas</a>) and emission between two conducting plates,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DowlingScully1991_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-DowlingScully1991-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seeley1993_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seeley1993-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MilonniKnight1973_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-MilonniKnight1973-60">[60]</a></sup> which is a problem of great interest due to the <a href="/wiki/Casimir_effect" title="Casimir effect">Casimir effect</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Agarwal2000_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Agarwal2000-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Evans2003_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Evans2003-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FicekSwain2005_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FicekSwain2005-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hypotheses">Hypotheses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=RF_resonant_cavity_thruster&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Hypotheses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>No mainstream scientific theory explains why such devices should produce thrust, however various attempts have been made to explain reported thrust measurements by the inventors and replicators.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Noise.2C_experimental_or_measurement_error">Noise, experimental or measurement error</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=RF_resonant_cavity_thruster&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Noise, experimental or measurement error">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The simplest explanation is that any thrust detected is due to experimental error or noise. In all of the experiments set up, a very large amount of energy is going into generating a tiny amount of thrust. Even the smallest stray signal – for instance from thermal or magnetic effects – could produce what looks like a thrust of that size. These experiments require more shielding from their environment than any of the experiments as of 2015 were able to provide, as each of those experimenters has noted. The strongest early result, from Yang's group in Xian, was later reported to be caused by a large experimental error.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yang_2016_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Yang_2016-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 2016 Eagleworks paper discusses nine possible sources of experimental error.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nat_Geo_Peer_review_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nat_Geo_Peer_review-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-discover_hurdle_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-discover_hurdle-64">[64]</a></sup> For example, one possible source of error comes from the thermal expansion of the thruster's heat sink, which is offset from the device’s center of gravity; as it expands, it causes the resonant cavity to move. The authors attempt to compensate for that, but doing so incorrectly would produce erroneous results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-discover_hurdle_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-discover_hurdle-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>A similar explanation is that imprecisions in measurement, variation in measurement, or publication bias, have led to a few positive observations with no statistical significance, while most negative observations were thrown out without being reported. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radiation_pressure">Radiation pressure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=RF_resonant_cavity_thruster&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Radiation pressure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Shawyer has suggested thrust is caused by a <a href="/wiki/Radiation_pressure" title="Radiation pressure">radiation pressure</a> imbalance between the two faces of the cavity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yang_2013_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Yang_2013-42">[42]</a></sup> He gave a presentation on this at the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronautical_Congress" title="International Astronautical Congress">International Astronautical Congress</a> 2014, later publishing it in the peer-reviewed Acta Astronautica.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shawyer_Acta_Astronautica_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shawyer_Acta_Astronautica-33">[33]</a></sup> In it he wrote, <i>In an EmDrive engine, microwave energy is converted to mechanical force according to the thrust equation, derived from the basic radiation pressure equation: F= 2 P<sub>0</sub> / c</i>. Shawyer's thrust equation, derived from Allen Cullen's equations,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cullen_1952_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cullen_1952-65">[65]</a></sup> is given by:</p>
